     </table>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">
       21st century
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 21st century">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Sandy_Flooding_Avenue_C_2012.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Hurricane_Sandy_Flooding_Avenue_C_2012.JPG/220px-Hurricane_Sandy_Flooding_Avenue_C_2012.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Hurricane_Sandy_Flooding_Avenue_C_2012.JPG/330px-Hurricane_Sandy_Flooding_Avenue_C_2012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Hurricane_Sandy_Flooding_Avenue_C_2012.JPG/440px-Hurricane_Sandy_Flooding_Avenue_C_2012.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Sandy_Flooding_Avenue_C_2012.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Flooding on
        <a href="/wiki/Avenue_C_(Manhattan)" title="Avenue C (Manhattan)">
         Avenue C
        </a>
        caused by
        <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Sandy" title="Hurricane Sandy">
         Hurricane Sandy
        </a>
        on October 29, 2012
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
         <a href="#cite_note-86">
          [84]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On
      <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">
       September 11, 2001
      </a>
      , two of four hijacked planes were flown into the
      <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">
       Twin Towers of the original World Trade Center
      </a>
      , and the towers subsequently collapsed.
      <a href="/wiki/7_World_Trade_Center" title="7 World Trade Center">
       7 World Trade Center
      </a>
      collapsed due to fires and structural damage caused by heavy debris falling from the collapse of the Twin Towers. The other buildings within the World Trade Center complex were damaged beyond repair and soon after demolished. The collapse of the Twin Towers caused extensive damage to other surrounding buildings and skyscrapers in Lower Manhattan, and resulted in the deaths of 2,606 people, in addition to those on the planes. Since 2001, most of Lower Manhattan has been restored, but
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_rebuilding_controversy" title="World Trade Center rebuilding controversy">
       there has been controversy
      </a>
      surrounding the rebuilding. However, many rescue workers and residents of the area
      <a href="/wiki/Health_effects_arising_from_the_September_11_attacks" title="Health effects arising from the September 11 attacks">
       developed
      </a>
      several life-threatening illnesses that have led to some of their subsequent deaths.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_September_11_Memorial_%26_Museum_at_the_World_Trade_Center" title="National September 11 Memorial &amp; Museum at the World Trade Center">
       A memorial at the site
      </a>
      was opened to the public on September 11, 2011, and the museum opened in 2014. In 2014, the new
      <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center" title="One World Trade Center">
       One World Trade Center
      </a>
      , at 1,776 feet (541 m) and formerly known as the Freedom Tower, became the tallest building in the Western Hemisphere,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [86]
       </a>
      </sup>
      while other skyscrapers were under construction at the site.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Occupy_Wall_Street" title="Occupy Wall Street">
       Occupy Wall Street
      </a>
      protests in
      <a href="/wiki/Zuccotti_Park" title="Zuccotti Park">
       Zuccotti Park
      </a>
      in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Financial_District_(Manhattan)" title="Financial District (Manhattan)">
       Financial District
      </a>
      of Lower Manhattan began on September 17, 2011, receiving global attention and spawning the
      <a href="/wiki/Occupy_movement" title="Occupy movement">
       Occupy
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Social_movement" title="Social movement">
       movement
      </a>
      against
      <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">
       social
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">
       economic inequality
      </a>
      worldwide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [87]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On October 29 and 30, 2012,
      <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Sandy" title="Hurricane Sandy">
       Hurricane Sandy
      </a>
      caused
      <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_Hurricane_Sandy_in_New_York" title="Effects of Hurricane Sandy in New York">
       extensive destruction
      </a>
      in the borough, ravaging portions of Lower Manhattan with record-high
      <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">
       storm surge
      </a>
      from New York Harbor,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYC_after_Sandy_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYC_after_Sandy-90">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
      severe flooding, and high winds, causing
      <a href="/wiki/Power_outage" title="Power outage">
       power outages
      </a>
      for hundreds of thousands of city residents
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and leading to
      <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">
       gasoline
      </a>
      shortages
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and disruption of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_transit" title="Mass transit">
       mass transit
      </a>
      systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The storm and its profound impacts have prompted the discussion of constructing
      <a href="/wiki/Seawall" title="Seawall">
       seawalls
      </a>
      and other
      <a href="/wiki/Coastal_management" title="Coastal management">
       coastal barriers
      </a>
      around the shorelines of the borough and the metropolitan area to minimize the risk of destructive consequences from another such event in the future.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        [95]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
       Geography
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geography">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_New_York_City" title="Geography of New York City">
       Geography of New York City
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:376px;max-width:376px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:168px;max-width:168px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NYC-GRID-1811.png">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="750" height="478" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NYC-GRID-1811.png/166px-NYC-GRID-1811.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NYC-GRID-1811.png/249px-NYC-GRID-1811.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NYC-GRID-1811.png/332px-NYC-GRID-1811.png 2x" width="166"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Modern redrawing of 1807 version of Commissioner's Grid plan for Manhattan, a few years before 1811 adoption.
         <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">
          Central Park
         </a>
         is absent. Dark color denotes existing blocks, light gray were planned.
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:204px;max-width:204px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA_Manhattan.jpg">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="1431" data-file-width="604" height="479" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/NASA_Manhattan.jpg/202px-NASA_Manhattan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/NASA_Manhattan.jpg/303px-NASA_Manhattan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/NASA_Manhattan.jpg/404px-NASA_Manhattan.jpg 2x" width="202"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">
          Central Park
         </a>
         in the center of satellite image. Manhattan is bound by
         <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">
          Hudson River
         </a>
         to the west,
         <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River" title="Harlem River">
          Harlem River
         </a>
         to the north, and
         <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">
          East River
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
       <div style="clear:left">
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Components">
       Components
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Components">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The borough consists of Manhattan Island, Marble Hill, and several small islands, including
      <a href="/wiki/Randalls_and_Wards_Islands" title="Randalls and Wards Islands">
       Randalls Island and Wards Island
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Island" title="Roosevelt Island">
       Roosevelt Island
      </a>
      in the East River, and
      <a href="/wiki/Governors_Island" title="Governors Island">
       Governors Island
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Island" title="Liberty Island">
       Liberty Island
      </a>
      to the south in
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_Harbor" title="New York Harbor">
       New York Harbor
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islands_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Islands-98">
        [96]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Census_Bureau" title="U.S. Census Bureau">
       U.S. Census Bureau
      </a>
      , New York County has a total area of 33.6 square miles (87 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ), of which 22.8 square miles (59 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) is land and 10.8 square miles (28 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) (32%) is water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GR1_99-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GR1-99">
        [97]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The northern segment of Upper Manhattan represents a geographic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panhandle" title="Panhandle">
       panhandle
      </a>
      . Manhattan Island is 22.7 square miles (59 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) in area, 13.4 miles (21.6 km) long and 2.3 miles (3.7 km) wide, at its widest (near
      <a href="/wiki/14th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="14th Street (Manhattan)">
       14th Street
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuff_100-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stuff-100">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Manhattan_Island">
       Manhattan Island
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Manhattan Island">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Manhattan Island is loosely divided into Downtown (
      <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">
       Lower Manhattan
      </a>
      ), Midtown (
      <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">
       Midtown Manhattan
      </a>
      ), and Uptown (
      <a href="/wiki/Upper_Manhattan" title="Upper Manhattan">
       Upper Manhattan
      </a>
      ), with
      <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">
       Fifth Avenue
      </a>
      dividing Manhattan's east and west sides. Manhattan Island is bounded by the
      <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">
       Hudson River
      </a>
      to the west and the
      <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">
       East River
      </a>
      to the east. To the north, the
      <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River" title="Harlem River">
       Harlem River
      </a>
      divides Manhattan Island from
      <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">
       the Bronx
      </a>
      and the mainland United States.
     </p>
     <p>
      Early in the 19th century,
      <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">
       landfill
      </a>
      was used to expand Lower Manhattan from the natural Hudson shoreline at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenwich_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Greenwich Street (Manhattan)">
       Greenwich Street
      </a>
      to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Street_(Manhattan)" title="West Street (Manhattan)">
       West Street
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        [99]
       </a>
      </sup>
      When
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Building_of_the_World_Trade_Center" title="Building of the World Trade Center">
       building the World Trade Center
      </a>
      in 1968, 1.2 million cubic yards (917,000 m³) of material was excavated from the site.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gillespie-p71_102-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gillespie-p71-102">
        [100]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rather than dumping the spoil at sea or in landfills, the fill material was used to expand the Manhattan shoreline across West Street, creating
      <a href="/wiki/Battery_Park_City" title="Battery Park City">
       Battery Park City
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iglauer_103-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-iglauer-103">
        [101]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The result was a 700-foot (210-m) extension into the river, running six blocks or 1,484 feet (452 m), covering 92 acres (37 ha), providing a 1.2-mile (1.9 km) riverfront esplanade and over 30 acres (12 ha) of parks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        [102]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Marble_Hill">
       Marble Hill
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Marble Hill">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      One neighborhood of New York County is contiguous with the mainland.
      <a href="/wiki/Marble_Hill,_Manhattan" title="Marble Hill, Manhattan">
       Marble Hill
      </a>
      at one time was part of Manhattan Island, but the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harlem_River_Ship_Canal" title="Harlem River Ship Canal">
       Harlem River Ship Canal
      </a>
      , dug in 1895 to improve navigation on the Harlem River, separated it from the remainder of Manhattan as an island between the Bronx and the remainder of Manhattan.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canal_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-canal-105">
        [103]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before World War I, the section of the original Harlem River channel separating Marble Hill from The Bronx was filled in, and Marble Hill became part of the mainland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-106">
        [104]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Marble Hill is one example of how Manhattan's land has been considerably altered by human intervention. The borough has seen substantial
      <a href="/wiki/Land_reclamation" title="Land reclamation">
       land reclamation
      </a>
      along its waterfronts since Dutch colonial times, and much of the natural variation in its
      <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">
       topography
      </a>
      has been evened out.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mannahatta_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mannahatta-35">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Smaller_islands">
       Smaller islands
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Smaller islands">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_smaller_islands_in_New_York_City" title="List of smaller islands in New York City">
       List of smaller islands in New York City
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_Harbor" title="New York Harbor">
       New York Harbor
      </a>
      there are three smaller islands:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Ellis_Island" title="Ellis Island">
        Ellis Island
       </a>
       , shared with
       <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">
        New Jersey
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Governors_Island" title="Governors Island">
        Governors Island
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Island" title="Liberty Island">
        Liberty Island
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Other smaller islands, in the
      <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">
       East River
      </a>
      , include (from north to south):
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Randalls_and_Wards_Islands" title="Randalls and Wards Islands">
        Randalls and Wards Islands
       </a>
       , joined by landfill
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Mill_Rock" title="Mill Rock">
        Mill Rock
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Island" title="Roosevelt Island">
        Roosevelt Island
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/U_Thant_Island" title="U Thant Island">
        U Thant Island
       </a>
       (legally Belmont Island)
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">
       Geology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Geology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bedrock">
       Bedrock
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bedrock">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NY-Central-Park-Rock-7333.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/NY-Central-Park-Rock-7333.jpg/250px-NY-Central-Park-Rock-7333.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/NY-Central-Park-Rock-7333.jpg/375px-NY-Central-Park-Rock-7333.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/NY-Central-Park-Rock-7333.jpg/500px-NY-Central-Park-Rock-7333.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NY-Central-Park-Rock-7333.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Manhattan schist outcropping in Central Park
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">
       bedrock
      </a>
      underlying much of Manhattan is a
      <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">
       mica
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Schist" title="Schist">
       schist
      </a>
      known as
      <i>
       Manhattan schist
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        [105]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is a strong, competent
      <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">
       metamorphic rock
      </a>
      created when
      <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">
       Pangaea
      </a>
      formed. It is well suited for the foundations of tall buildings. In
      <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">
       Central Park
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Outcrop" title="Outcrop">
       outcrops
      </a>
      of Manhattan Schist occur and
      <a href="/wiki/Rat_Rock" title="Rat Rock">
       Rat Rock
      </a>
      is one rather large example.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        [106]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        [107]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        [108]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Geologically, a predominant feature of the substrata of Manhattan is that the underlying bedrock base of the island rises considerably closer to the surface near Midtown Manhattan, dips down lower between
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/29th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="29th Street (Manhattan)">
       29th Street
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Canal_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Canal Street (Manhattan)">
       Canal Street
      </a>
      , then rises toward the surface again in Lower Manhattan. It has been widely believed that the depth to bedrock was the primary underlying reason for the clustering of skyscrapers in the Midtown and Financial District areas, and their absence over the intervening territory between these two areas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        [109]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        [110]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, research has shown that economic factors played a bigger part in the locations of these skyscrapers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        [111]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        [112]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        [113]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Updated_seismic_analysis">
       Updated seismic analysis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Updated seismic analysis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      According to the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">
       United States Geological Survey
      </a>
      , an updated analysis of
      <a href="/wiki/Seismic_hazard" title="Seismic hazard">
       seismic hazard
      </a>
      in July 2014 revealed a "slightly lower hazard for tall buildings" in Manhattan than previously assessed. Scientists estimated this lessened risk based upon a lower likelihood than previously thought of slow shaking near New York City, which would be more likely to cause damage to taller structures from an earthquake in the vicinity of the city.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        [114]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Locations">
       Locations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Locations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liberty_Island_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg">
        <img alt="A tall green statue on an island in a harbor." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="4800" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Liberty_Island_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/250px-Liberty_Island_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Liberty_Island_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/375px-Liberty_Island_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Liberty_Island_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg/500px-Liberty_Island_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liberty_Island_photo_D_Ramey_Logan.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Island" title="Liberty Island">
         Liberty Island
        </a>
        is an
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exclave" title="Exclave">
         exclave
        </a>
        of Manhattan, of New York City, and of New York State, surrounded by New Jersey waters.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Adjacent_counties">
       Adjacent counties
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

       </span>

       </a>

      </li>

      </li>

       <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">

       </a>
       ) — south and southeast
      </li>
      <li>
       Richmond County (
       <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island" title="Staten Island">

       </a>
       ) — southwest
      </li>

     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neighborhoods">
       Neighborhoods
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Neighborhoods">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Neighborhoods_in_New_York_City" title="Neighborhoods in New York City">
       Neighborhoods in New York City
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Manhattan_neighborhoods" title="List of Manhattan neighborhoods">
       List of Manhattan neighborhoods
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Manhattan's many neighborhoods are not named according to any particular convention. Some are geographical (the
      <a href="/wiki/Upper_East_Side" title="Upper East Side">
       Upper East Side
      </a>
      ), or ethnically descriptive (
      <a href="/wiki/Little_Italy,_Manhattan" title="Little Italy, Manhattan">
       Little Italy
      </a>
      ). Others are
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acronyms" title="Acronyms">
       acronyms
      </a>
      , such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TriBeCa" title="TriBeCa">
       TriBeCa
      </a>
      (for "TRIangle BElow CAnal Street") or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SoHo" title="SoHo">
       SoHo
      </a>
      ("SOuth of HOuston"), or the far more recent vintages
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NoLIta,_Manhattan" title="NoLIta, Manhattan">
       NoLIta
      </a>
      ("NOrth of Little ITAly").
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        [115]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        [116]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NoMad" title="NoMad">
       NoMad
      </a>
      ("NOrth of MADison Square Park").
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        [117]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        [118]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        [119]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Harlem" title="Harlem">
       Harlem
      </a>
      is a name from the Dutch colonial era after
      <a href="/wiki/Haarlem" title="Haarlem">
       Haarlem
      </a>
      , a city in the Netherlands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        [120]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Alphabet_City,_Manhattan" title="Alphabet City, Manhattan">
       Alphabet City
      </a>
      comprises
      <a href="/wiki/Avenue_A_(Manhattan)" title="Avenue A (Manhattan)">
       Avenues A
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Avenue_B_(Manhattan)" title="Avenue B (Manhattan)">
       B
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Avenue_C_(Manhattan)" title="Avenue C (Manhattan)">
       C
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Avenue_D_(Manhattan)" title="Avenue D (Manhattan)">
       D
      </a>
      , to which its name refers. Some have simple
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folkloric" title="Folkloric">
       folkloric
      </a>
      names, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Hell%27s_Kitchen,_Manhattan" title="Hell's Kitchen, Manhattan">
       Hell's Kitchen
      </a>
      , alongside their more official but lesser used title (in this case, Clinton).
     </p>
     <p>
      Some neighborhoods, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SoHo" title="SoHo">
       SoHo
      </a>
      , are commercial and known for
      <a href="/wiki/Luxury_goods" title="Luxury goods">
       upscale shopping
      </a>
      . Others, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Village" title="Greenwich Village">
       Greenwich Village
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_East_Side,_Manhattan" title="Lower East Side, Manhattan">
       Lower East Side
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Alphabet_City,_Manhattan" title="Alphabet City, Manhattan">
       Alphabet City
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/East_Village,_Manhattan" title="East Village, Manhattan">
       East Village
      </a>
      , have long been associated with the
      <a href="/wiki/Bohemianism" title="Bohemianism">
       Bohemian
      </a>
      subculture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        [121]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_Manhattan" title="Chelsea, Manhattan">
       Chelsea
      </a>
      is a neighborhood with a large gay population, and recently a center of New York's art industry and nightlife.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        [122]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_Heights,_Manhattan" title="Washington Heights, Manhattan">
       Washington Heights
      </a>
      is a vibrant neighborhood of immigrants from the
      <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">
       Dominican Republic
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Manhattan" title="Chinatown, Manhattan">
       Chinatown
      </a>
      has a dense population of people of Chinese descent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        [123]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        [124]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Koreatown,_Manhattan" title="Koreatown, Manhattan">
       Koreatown
      </a>
      is roughly bounded by 5th and 6th Avenues, between 31st and 36th Streets.
      <a href="/wiki/Rose_Hill,_Manhattan" title="Rose Hill, Manhattan">
       Rose Hill
      </a>
      features a growing number of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curry_Hill" title="Curry Hill">
       Indian
      </a>
      restaurants and spice shops along a stretch of
      <a href="/wiki/Lexington_Avenue" title="Lexington Avenue">
       Lexington Avenue
      </a>
      between 25th and 30th Streets which has become known as
      <i>
       Curry Hill
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        [125]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In Manhattan,
      <i>
       uptown
      </i>
      means north (more precisely north-northeast, which is the direction the island and its street grid system are oriented) and
      <i>
       downtown
      </i>
      means south (south-southwest).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        [126]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This usage differs from that of most American cities, where
      <i>
       downtown
      </i>
      refers to the central business district. Manhattan has two central business districts, the
      <a href="/wiki/Financial_District,_Manhattan" title="Financial District, Manhattan">
       Financial District
      </a>
      at the southern tip of the island, and
      <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">
       Midtown Manhattan
      </a>
      . The term
      <i>
       uptown
      </i>
      also refers to the northern part of Manhattan above
      <a href="/wiki/72nd_Street_(Manhattan)" title="72nd Street (Manhattan)">
       72nd Street
      </a>
      and
      <i>
       downtown
      </i>
      to the southern portion below
      <a href="/wiki/14th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="14th Street (Manhattan)">
       14th Street
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYCBasics_129-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYCBasics-129">
        [127]
       </a>
      </sup>
      with
      <i>
       Midtown
      </i>
      covering the area in between, though definitions can be rather fluid depending on the situation.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">
       Fifth Avenue
      </a>
      roughly bisects Manhattan Island and acts as the demarcation line for east/west designations (e.g., East 27th Street, West 42nd Street); street addresses start at Fifth Avenue and increase heading away from Fifth Avenue, at a rate of 100 per block on most streets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYCBasics_129-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYCBasics-129">
        [127]
       </a>
      </sup>
      South of
      <a href="/wiki/Waverly_Place" title="Waverly Place">
       Waverly Place
      </a>
      , Fifth Avenue terminates and Broadway becomes the east/west demarcation line. Though the grid does start with 1st Street, just north of
      <a href="/wiki/Houston_Street" title="Houston Street">
       Houston Street
      </a>
      (the southernmost street divided in west and east portions; pronounced HOW-stin), the grid does not fully take hold until north of
      <a href="/wiki/14th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="14th Street (Manhattan)">
       14th Street
      </a>
      , where nearly all east-west streets are numerically identified, which increase from south to north to 220th Street, the highest numbered street on the island. Streets in Midtown are usually one way with a few exceptions (14th, 23rd, 34th, and 42nd for example). The rule of thumb is that odd-numbered streets run west, while even-numbered streets run east.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuff_100-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stuff-100">
        [98]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
       Climate
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Climate">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Under the
      <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">
       Köppen climate classification
      </a>
      , using the 0 °C (32 °F) coldest month (January)
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotherm_(contour_line)" title="Isotherm (contour line)">
       isotherm
      </a>
      , New York City including Manhattan itself experiences a
      <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">
       humid subtropical climate
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       Cfa
      </i>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peel.2C_M._C..3B_Finlayson.2C_B._L..3B_McMahon.2C_T._A_130-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Peel.2C_M._C..3B_Finlayson.2C_B._L..3B_McMahon.2C_T._A-130">
        [128]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorkpolonia.com_131-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-newyorkpolonia.com-131">
        [129]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and located at around 40°N latitude, is the northernmost major city on the North American continent with this categorization. Winters are cold and damp, and prevailing wind patterns that blow offshore minimize the moderating effects of the Atlantic Ocean; yet the Atlantic and the partial shielding from colder air by the
      <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">
       Appalachians
      </a>
      keep the city warmer in the winter than inland North American cities at similar or lesser latitudes, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">
       Pittsburgh
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati" title="Cincinnati">
       Cincinnati
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis" title="Indianapolis">
       Indianapolis
      </a>
      , helping to moderate the amount of snow, which averages 25 inches (64 cm) each year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Weather_Service_Climate_132-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-National_Weather_Service_Climate-132">
        [130]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Spring and fall in New York City are mild, while summer is very warm and humid, with temperatures of 90 °F (32 °C) or higher recorded from 18 to 25 days on average during the season.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYC_climate_133-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYC_climate-133">
        [131]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The city's long-term climate patterns are affected by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_Multidecadal_Oscillation" title="Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation">
       Atlantic Multidecadal Oscillation
      </a>
      , a 70-year-long warming and cooling cycle in the Atlantic that influences the frequency and severity of hurricanes and coastal storms in the region.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        [132]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Central Park is the location of record for the borough's climatic data.
     </p>
     <p>
      Temperature records have been set as high as 106 °F (41 °C) on July 9, 1936, and as low as −15 °F (−26 °C) on February 9, 1934.
     </p>
     <p>
      Summer evening temperatures are elevated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">
       urban heat island
      </a>
      effect, which causes heat absorbed during the day to be radiated back at night, raising temperatures by as much as 7 °F (4 °C) when winds are slow.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        [133]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <br/>
     </p>

      <span class="mw-editsection">

      <div class="thumbinner" style="">
       <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;direction:rtl;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WestsideManhattanPanorama.jpg" title="The skyscrapers of New York City are almost all situated in Manhattan, seen here in this panorama viewed from Weehawken, New Jersey, in January 2015. Prominent tall buildings include One57 and 432 Park Avenue, left of center; the Empire State Building, right of center; and on the far right of the picture, One World Trade Center. Near the last mentioned, Four World Trade Center, 70 Pine Street, the Woolworth Building, and 40 Wall Street can be seen. At the center of the skyline picture, the Chrysler Building, The New York Times Building, and the Condé Nast Building can be picked out of the crowd by their spires. Upper Manhattan (Uptown) is left (north), Midtown Manhattan (Midtown) at center, and Lower Manhattan (Downtown) is right (south). (January 2015)">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="702" data-file-width="6691" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/WestsideManhattanPanorama.jpg/1500px-WestsideManhattanPanorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/WestsideManhattanPanorama.jpg/2250px-WestsideManhattanPanorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/WestsideManhattanPanorama.jpg/3000px-WestsideManhattanPanorama.jpg 2x" width="1500"/>
        </a>

        <div align="center">
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">
          skyscrapers
         </a>
         of New York City are almost all situated in Manhattan, seen here in this panorama viewed from
         <a href="/wiki/Weehawken,_New_Jersey" title="Weehawken, New Jersey">
          Weehawken, New Jersey
         </a>
         , in January 2015. Prominent tall buildings include
         <a href="/wiki/One57" title="One57">
          One57
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/432_Park_Avenue" title="432 Park Avenue">
          432 Park Avenue
         </a>
         , left of center; the
         <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">
          Empire State Building
         </a>
         , right of center; and on the far right of the picture,
         <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center" title="One World Trade Center">
          One World Trade Center
         </a>
         . Near the last mentioned,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_World_Trade_Center" title="Four World Trade Center">
          Four World Trade Center
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/70_Pine_Street" title="70 Pine Street">
          70 Pine Street
         </a>
         , the
         <a href="/wiki/Woolworth_Building" title="Woolworth Building">
          Woolworth Building
         </a>
         , and
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/40_Wall_Street" title="40 Wall Street">
          40 Wall Street
         </a>
         can be seen. At the center of the
         <a href="/wiki/Skyline" title="Skyline">
          skyline
         </a>
         picture, the
         <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">
          Chrysler Building
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times_Building" title="The New York Times Building">
          The New York Times Building
         </a>
         , and the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cond%C3%A9_Nast_Building" title="Condé Nast Building">
          Condé Nast Building
         </a>
         can be picked out of the crowd by their
         <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">
          spires
         </a>
         .
         <a href="/wiki/Upper_Manhattan" title="Upper Manhattan">
          Upper Manhattan
         </a>

         <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">
          Midtown Manhattan
         </a>
         (Midtown) at center, and
         <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">

         </a>
         (Downtown) is right (south). (January 2015)

       <td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">
        −7.2%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th style="text-align:center;padding:1px;border-bottom:1px solid #bbbbbb">

       </td>
      </tr>

       </td>

       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <th style="text-align:center;padding:1px">

       </th>

       </td>

       </td>
      </tr>

     <p>
      At the
      <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">
       2010 Census
      </a>
      , there were 1,585,873 people living in Manhattan, an increase of 3.2% since 2000. Since 2010, Manhattan's population was estimated by the Census Bureau to have increased 3.7% to 1,644,518 as of 2015
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , representing 19.3% of New York City's population and 8.3% of New York State's population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ManhattanQuickFacts_1-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-ManhattanQuickFacts-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYCquickfacts_146-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYCquickfacts-146">
        [142]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of the 2000 Census, the population density of New York County was 66,940 per square mile (25,846/km²), the highest population density of any county in the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        [143]
       </a>
      </sup>
      If 2012 census estimates were accurate, the population density then approximated 70,518 people per square mile (27,227/km²). In 1910, at the height of European immigration to New York, Manhattan's population density reached a peak of 101,548 people per square mile (39,208/km²).
     </p>
     <p>
      According to 2012
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census" title="United States Census">
       Census estimates
      </a>
      , 65.2% of the population was
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_American" title="White American">
       White
      </a>
      , 18.4%
      <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">
       Black
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">
       African American
      </a>
      , 1.2%
      <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">
       American Indian
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaska_Native" title="Alaska Native">
       Alaska Native
      </a>
      , 12.0%
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_American" title="Asian American">
       Asian
      </a>
      , and 3.1% of two or more races. 25.8% of Manhattan's population was of
      <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">
       Hispanic or Latino
      </a>
      origin, of any race. Manhattan has the second highest percentage of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Hispanic_Whites" title="Non-Hispanic Whites">
       non-Hispanic Whites
      </a>
      (48%) of New York City's boroughs, after Staten Island (where non-Hispanic Whites make up 64% of residents).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ManhattanQuickFacts_1-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-ManhattanQuickFacts-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Department_of_City_Planning" title="New York City Department of City Planning">
       New York City Department of City Planning
      </a>
      projects that Manhattan's population will increase by 289,000 people between 2000 and 2030, an increase of 18.8% over the period, second only to Staten Island, while the rest of the city is projected to grow by 12.7% over the same period. The school-age population is expected to grow 4.4% by 2030, in contrast to a small decline in the city as a whole. The elderly population is forecast to grow by 57.9%, with the borough adding 108,000 persons ages 65 and over, compared to 44.2% growth citywide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        [144]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to the 2009
      <a href="/wiki/American_Community_Survey" title="American Community Survey">
       American Community Survey
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        [145]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the average household size was 2.11, and the average family size was 3.21. Approximately 59.4% of the population over the age of 25 have a bachelor's degree or higher. Approximately 27.0% of the population is foreign-born, and 61.7% of the population over the age of 5 speak only English at home. People of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irish_American" title="Irish American">
       Irish
      </a>
      ancestry make up 7.8% of the population, while Italian Americans make up 6.8% of the population.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_American" title="German American">
       German Americans
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_American" title="Russian American">
       Russian Americans
      </a>
      make up 7.2% and 6.2% of the population respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        [146]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2000, 56.4% of people living in Manhattan were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" title="Race (United States Census)">
       White
      </a>
      , 17.39% were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" title="Race (United States Census)">
       Black
      </a>
      , 14.14% were from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" title="Race (United States Census)">
       other races
      </a>
      , 9.40% were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" title="Race (United States Census)">
       Asian
      </a>
      , 0.5% were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" title="Race (United States Census)">
       Native American
      </a>
      , and 0.07% were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" title="Race (United States Census)">
       Pacific Islander
      </a>
      . 4.14% were from two or more races. 27.18% were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(United_States_Census)" title="Race (United States Census)">
       Hispanic
      </a>
      of any race.
     </p>
     <p>
      There were 738,644 households. 25.2% were married couples living together, 12.6% had a female householder with no husband present, and 59.1% were non-families. 17.1% had children under the age of 18 living with them. 48% of all households were made up of individuals and 10.9% had someone living alone who was 65 years of age or older. The average household size was two and the average family size was 2.99.
     </p>
     <p>
      Manhattan's population was spread out with 16.8% under the age of 18, 10.2% from 18 to 24, 38.3% from 25 to 44, 22.6% from 45 to 64, and 12.2% who were 65 years of age or older. The median age was 36 years. For every 100 females, there were 90.3 males. For every 100 females age 18 and over, there were 87.9 males.
     </p>
     <p>
      Manhattan is one of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highest-income_counties_in_the_United_States" title="Highest-income counties in the United States">
       highest-income places
      </a>
      in the United States with a population greater than one million. As of 2012
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , Manhattan's cost of living was the highest in the United States,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
       <a href="#cite_note-151">
        [147]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but the borough also contained the country's most profound level of income inequality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        [148]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Manhattan is also the United States county with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highest-income_counties_in_the_United_States#Per_capita_income" title="Highest-income counties in the United States">
       highest per capita income
      </a>
      , being the sole county whose
      <a href="/wiki/Personal_income_in_the_United_States" title="Personal income in the United States">
       per capita income
      </a>
      exceeded $100,000 in 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
       <a href="#cite_note-153">
        [149]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2012,
      <i>
       The New York Times
      </i>
      reported that "the income gap in Manhattan, already wider than almost anywhere else in the country, rivaled disparities in sub-Saharan Africa. ... The wealthiest fifth of Manhattanites made more than 40 times what the lowest fifth reported, a widening gap (it was 38 times, the year before) surpassed by only a few developing countries".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
       <a href="#cite_note-154">
        [150]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Lower Manhattan has been experiencing a
      <a href="/wiki/Baby_boom" title="Baby boom">
       baby boom
      </a>
      , well above the overall birth rate in Manhattan, with the area south of
      <a href="/wiki/Canal_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Canal Street (Manhattan)">
       Canal Street
      </a>
      witnessing 1,086 births in 2010, 12% greater than 2009 and over twice the number born in 2001.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
       <a href="#cite_note-155">
        [151]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Financial District alone has witnessed growth in its population to approximately 43,000 as of 2014
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , nearly double the 23,000 recorded at the 2000 Census.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
       <a href="#cite_note-156">
        [152]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">
       Religion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Religion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Manhattan is religiously diverse. The largest religious affiliation is the
      <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
       Catholic Church
      </a>
      , whose adherents constitute 564,505 persons (more than 36% of the population) and maintain 110 congregations.
      <a href="/wiki/American_Jews" title="American Jews">
       Jews
      </a>
      comprise the second largest religious group, with 314,500 persons (20.5%) in 102 congregations. They are followed by
      <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">
       Protestants
      </a>
      , with 139,732 adherents (9.1%) and
      <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
       Muslims
      </a>
      , with 37,078 (2.4%).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
       <a href="#cite_note-157">
        [153]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">
       Languages
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Languages">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      As of 2010
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , 59.98% (902,267) of Manhattan residents, ages five and older, spoke only English at home, while 23.07% (347,033) spoke Spanish, 5.33% (80,240) Chinese, 2.03% (30,567) French, 0.78% (11,776) Japanese, 0.77% (11,517) Russian, 0.72% (10,788) Korean, 0.70% (10,496) German, 0.66% (9,868) Italian, 0.64% (9,555) Hebrew, and 0.48% (7,158) African languages as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main_language" title="Main language">
       main language
      </a>
      . In total, 40.02% (602,058) of Manhattan's population, ages 5 and older, spoke a language other than English at home.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
       <a href="#cite_note-158">
        [154]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Landmarks_and_architecture">

      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">

       </span>

       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>

      </a>
     </div>

       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">

        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Park_Avenue" title="Park Avenue">
         Park Avenue
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/57th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="57th Street (Manhattan)">
         57th Street
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">
         Midtown Manhattan
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Theater_District,_Manhattan" title="Theater District, Manhattan">
       Theater District
      </a>
      around
      <a href="/wiki/Broadway_(Manhattan)" title="Broadway (Manhattan)">
       Broadway
      </a>
      at
      <a href="/wiki/Times_Square" title="Times Square">
       Times Square
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">
       New York University
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">
       Columbia University
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Flatiron_Building" title="Flatiron Building">
       Flatiron Building
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Financial_District,_Manhattan" title="Financial District, Manhattan">
       Financial District
      </a>
      around
      <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">
       Wall Street
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Center_for_the_Performing_Arts" title="Lincoln Center for the Performing Arts">
       Lincoln Center for the Performing Arts
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Little_Italy,_Manhattan" title="Little Italy, Manhattan">
       Little Italy
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harlem,_Manhattan" title="Harlem, Manhattan">
       Harlem
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">
       American Museum of Natural History
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Manhattan" title="Chinatown, Manhattan">
       Chinatown
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">
       Central Park
      </a>
      are all located on this densely populated island. Manhattan has many energy-efficient green office buildings, such as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hearst_Tower_(New_York_City)" title="Hearst Tower (New York City)">
       Hearst Tower
      </a>
      , the rebuilt
      <a href="/wiki/7_World_Trade_Center" title="7 World Trade Center">
       7 World Trade Center
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenbuilding_159-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-greenbuilding-159">
        [155]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_Tower_(Manhattan)" title="Bank of America Tower (Manhattan)">
       Bank of America Tower
      </a>
      —the first skyscraper designed to attain a Platinum
      <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">
       LEED
      </a>
      Certification.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
       <a href="#cite_note-160">
        [156]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
       <a href="#cite_note-161">
        [157]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Architectural_history">
       Architectural history
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Architectural history">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">
       skyscraper
      </a>
      , which has shaped Manhattan's distinctive
      <a href="/wiki/Skyline" title="Skyline">
       skyline
      </a>
      , has been closely associated with New York City's identity since the end of the 19th century. From 1890 to 1973, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="History of the tallest buildings in the world">
       world's tallest building
      </a>
      was in Manhattan (with a gap between 1901 and 1908, when the title was held by
      <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_City_Hall" title="Philadelphia City Hall">
       Philadelphia City Hall
      </a>
      ), with nine different buildings holding the title.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
       <a href="#cite_note-162">
        [158]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_World_Building" title="New York World Building">
       New York World Building
      </a>
      on
      <a href="/wiki/Park_Row_(Manhattan)" title="Park Row (Manhattan)">
       Park Row
      </a>
      , was the first to take the title in 1890, standing 309 feet (94 m) until 1955, when it was demolished to construct a new ramp to the
      <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Bridge" title="Brooklyn Bridge">
       Brooklyn Bridge
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
       <a href="#cite_note-163">
        [159]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The nearby
      <a href="/wiki/Park_Row_Building" title="Park Row Building">
       Park Row Building
      </a>
      , with its 29 stories standing 391 feet (119 m) high took the title in 1899.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
       <a href="#cite_note-164">
        [160]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The 41-story
      <a href="/wiki/Singer_Building" title="Singer Building">
       Singer Building
      </a>
      , constructed in 1908 as the headquarters of the eponymous sewing machine manufacturer, stood 612 feet (187 m) high until 1967, when it became the tallest building ever demolished.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
       <a href="#cite_note-165">
        [161]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Life_Insurance_Company_Tower" title="Metropolitan Life Insurance Company Tower">
       Metropolitan Life Insurance Company Tower
      </a>
      , standing 700 feet (210 m) at the foot of
      <a href="/wiki/Madison_Avenue" title="Madison Avenue">
       Madison Avenue
      </a>
      , wrested the title in 1909, with a tower reminiscent of
      <a href="/wiki/St_Mark%27s_Campanile" title="St Mark's Campanile">
       St Mark's Campanile
      </a>
      in Venice.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        [162]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Woolworth_Building" title="Woolworth Building">
       Woolworth Building
      </a>
      , and its distinctive
      <a href="/wiki/Gothic_architecture" title="Gothic architecture">
       Gothic architecture
      </a>
      , took the title in 1913, topping off at 792 feet (241 m).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
       <a href="#cite_note-167">
        [163]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Roaring_Twenties" title="Roaring Twenties">
       Roaring Twenties
      </a>
      saw a race to the sky, with three separate buildings pursuing the world's tallest title in the span of a year. As the stock market soared in the days before the
      <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Crash_of_1929" title="Wall Street Crash of 1929">
       Wall Street Crash of 1929
      </a>
      , two developers publicly competed for the crown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        [164]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At 927 feet (283 m),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/40_Wall_Street" title="40 Wall Street">
       40 Wall Street
      </a>
      , completed in May 1930 in an astonishing eleven months as the headquarters of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bank_of_Manhattan" title="Bank of Manhattan">
       Bank of Manhattan
      </a>
      , seemed to have secured the title.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        [165]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lexington_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Lexington Avenue (Manhattan)">
       Lexington Avenue
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/42nd_Street_(Manhattan)" title="42nd Street (Manhattan)">
       42nd Street
      </a>
      , auto executive
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_Chrysler" title="Walter Chrysler">
       Walter Chrysler
      </a>
      and his architect
      <a href="/wiki/William_Van_Alen" title="William Van Alen">
       William Van Alen
      </a>
      developed plans to build the structure's trademark 185-foot (56 m) high spire in secret, pushing the
      <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">
       Chrysler Building
      </a>
      to 1,046 feet (319 m) and making it the tallest in the world when it was completed in 1929.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
       <a href="#cite_note-170">
        [166]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Both buildings were soon surpassed, with the May 1931 completion of the 102-story
      <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">
       Empire State Building
      </a>
      with its
      <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">
       Art Deco
      </a>
      tower soaring 1,250 feet (380 m) to the top of the building. The 203-foot (62 m) high pinnacle was later added bringing the total height of the building to 1,453 ft (443 m).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
       <a href="#cite_note-171">
        [167]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
       <a href="#cite_note-172">
        [168]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The former Twin Towers of the World Trade Center were located in Lower Manhattan. At 1,368 and 1,362 feet (417 and 415 m), the 110-story buildings were the world's tallest from 1972, until they were surpassed by the construction of the
      <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">
       Willis Tower
      </a>
      in 1974 (formerly known as the Sears Tower, located in
      <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">
       Chicago
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
       <a href="#cite_note-173">
        [169]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center" title="One World Trade Center">
       One World Trade Center
      </a>
      , a replacement for the Twin Towers of the World Trade Center, is currently the tallest building in the
      <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">
       Western Hemisphere
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
       <a href="#cite_note-174">
        [170]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1961, the
      <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Railroad" title="Pennsylvania Railroad">
       Pennsylvania Railroad
      </a>
      unveiled plans to tear down the old
      <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(1910%E2%80%931963)" title="Pennsylvania Station (1910–1963)">
       Penn Station
      </a>
      and replace it with a new
      <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">
       Madison Square Garden
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/One_Penn_Plaza" title="One Penn Plaza">
       office building
      </a>
      complex. Organized protests were aimed at preserving the
      <a href="/wiki/McKim,_Mead_%26_White" title="McKim, Mead &amp; White">
       McKim, Mead &amp; White
      </a>
      -designed structure completed in 1910, widely considered a masterpiece of the
      <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">
       Beaux-Arts
      </a>
      style and one of the architectural jewels of New York City.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
       <a href="#cite_note-175">
        [171]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite these efforts, demolition of the structure began in October 1963. The loss of Penn Station—called "an act of irresponsible public vandalism" by historian
      <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Mumford" title="Lewis Mumford">
       Lewis Mumford
      </a>
      —led directly to the enactment in 1965 of a local law establishing the
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Landmarks_Preservation_Commission" title="New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission">
       New York City Landmarks Preservation Commission
      </a>
      , which is responsible for preserving the "city's historic, aesthetic, and cultural heritage".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
       <a href="#cite_note-176">
        [172]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Historic_preservation" title="Historic preservation">
       historic preservation
      </a>
      movement triggered by Penn Station's demise has been credited with the retention of some one million structures nationwide, including nearly 1,000 in New York City.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
       <a href="#cite_note-177">
        [173]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Parkland">
       Parkland
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Parkland">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      17.8% of the borough, a total of 2,686 acres (10.87 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ), is devoted to parkland. Almost 70% of Manhattan's space devoted to parks is located outside of Central Park, including 204 playgrounds, 251 Greenstreets, 371 basketball courts and many other amenities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MBPOEnvironment_178-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MBPOEnvironment-178">
        [174]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">
       Central Park
      </a>
      is bordered on the north by West
      <a href="/wiki/110th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="110th Street (Manhattan)">
       110th Street
      </a>
      , on the west by
      <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Eighth Avenue (Manhattan)">
       Eighth Avenue
      </a>
      , on the south by West
      <a href="/wiki/59th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="59th Street (Manhattan)">
       59th Street
      </a>
      , and on the east by Fifth Avenue. Along the park's borders, these streets are usually referred to as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Park_North" title="Central Park North">
       Central Park North
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Park_West" title="Central Park West">
       Central Park West
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Park_South" title="Central Park South">
       Central Park South
      </a>
      , respectively (Fifth Avenue retains its name along the eastern border). The park was designed by
      <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Law_Olmsted" title="Frederick Law Olmsted">
       Frederick Law Olmsted
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Calvert_Vaux" title="Calvert Vaux">
       Calvert Vaux
      </a>
      . The 843-acre (3.41 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) park offers extensive
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walking_track" title="Walking track">
       walking tracks
      </a>
      , two
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice-skating" title="Ice-skating">
       ice-skating
      </a>
      rinks, a wildlife sanctuary, and grassy areas used for various sporting pursuits, as well as playgrounds for children. The park is a popular oasis for migrating birds, and thus is popular with bird watchers. The 6-mile (9.7 km) road circling the park is popular with joggers, bicyclists and inline skaters, especially on weekends and in the evenings after 7:00 pm, when automobile traffic is banned.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CentralPark_179-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CentralPark-179">
        [175]
       </a>
      </sup>
      While much of the park looks natural, it is almost entirely landscaped and contains several artificial lakes. The construction of Central Park in the 1850s was one of the era's most massive public works projects. Some 20,000 workers crafted the topography to create the English-style pastoral landscape Olmsted and Vaux sought to create. Workers moved nearly 3,000,000 cubic yards (2,300,000 m
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      ) of soil and planted more than 270,000 trees and shrubs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        [176]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/African_Burial_Ground_National_Monument" title="African Burial Ground National Monument">
       African Burial Ground National Monument
      </a>
      at Duane Street preserves a site containing the remains of over 400 Africans buried during the 17th and 18th centuries. The remains were found in 1991 during the construction of the
      <a href="/wiki/Foley_Square" title="Foley Square">
       Foley Square
      </a>
      Federal Office Building.
     </p>
     <table style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em;margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto">
