        </a>
        in the foreground and the
        <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Bridge" title="Manhattan Bridge">
         Manhattan Bridge
        </a>
        beyond it, are two of the three bridges that connect
        <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">
         Lower Manhattan
        </a>
        with
        <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">
         Brooklyn
        </a>
        over the
        <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">
         East River
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Storm_at_Manhattan.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Storm_at_Manhattan.jpg/250px-Storm_at_Manhattan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Storm_at_Manhattan.jpg/375px-Storm_at_Manhattan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Storm_at_Manhattan.jpg/500px-Storm_at_Manhattan.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Storm_at_Manhattan.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8th_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="8th Avenue (Manhattan)">
         8th Avenue
        </a>
        , looking northward ("Uptown"), in the rain. Most streets and avenues in Manhattan's
        <a href="/wiki/Commissioners%27_Plan_of_1811" title="Commissioners' Plan of 1811">
         grid plan
        </a>
        incorporate a
        <a href="/wiki/One-way_traffic" title="One-way traffic">
         one-way traffic
        </a>
        configuration.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manhattanhenge_in_june_2005.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Manhattanhenge_in_june_2005.jpg/250px-Manhattanhenge_in_june_2005.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Manhattanhenge_in_june_2005.jpg/375px-Manhattanhenge_in_june_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Manhattanhenge_in_june_2005.jpg/500px-Manhattanhenge_in_june_2005.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manhattanhenge_in_june_2005.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Manhattanhenge" title="Manhattanhenge">
         Manhattanhenge
        </a>
        , as seen looking westward at sunset in June 2005
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_numbered_streets_in_Manhattan" title="List of numbered streets in Manhattan">
       List of numbered streets in Manhattan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_eponymous_streets_in_New_York_City" title="List of eponymous streets in New York City">
       List of eponymous streets in New York City
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Commissioners%27_Plan_of_1811" title="Commissioners' Plan of 1811">
       Commissioners' Plan of 1811
      </a>
      called for twelve numbered avenues running north and south roughly parallel to the shore of the
      <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">
       Hudson River
      </a>
      , each 100 feet (30 m) wide, with
      <a href="/wiki/First_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="First Avenue (Manhattan)">
       First Avenue
      </a>
      on the east side and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelfth_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Twelfth Avenue (Manhattan)">
       Twelfth Avenue
      </a>
      on the west side. There are several intermittent avenues east of First Avenue, including four additional lettered avenues running from
      <a href="/wiki/Avenue_A_(Manhattan)" title="Avenue A (Manhattan)">
       Avenue A
      </a>
      eastward to
      <a href="/wiki/Avenue_D_(Manhattan)" title="Avenue D (Manhattan)">
       Avenue D
      </a>
      in an area now known as
      <a href="/wiki/Alphabet_City,_Manhattan" title="Alphabet City, Manhattan">
       Alphabet City
      </a>
      in Manhattan's
      <a href="/wiki/East_Village,_Manhattan" title="East Village, Manhattan">
       East Village
      </a>
      . The numbered streets in Manhattan run east-west, and are generally 60 feet (18 m) wide, with about 200 feet (61 m) between each pair of streets. With each combined street and block adding up to about 260 feet (79 m), there are almost exactly 20 blocks per mile. The typical block in Manhattan is 250 by 600 feet (76 by 183 m).
     </p>
     <p>
      According to the original Commissioner's Plan, there were
      <a href="/wiki/155th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="155th Street (Manhattan)">
       155
      </a>
      numbered crosstown streets,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307">
       <a href="#cite_note-307">
        [303]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but later the grid was extended up to the northernmost corner of Manhattan, where the last numbered street is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/220th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="220th Street (Manhattan)">
       220th Street (Manhattan)
      </a>
      . Moreover, the numbering system continues even in
      <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">
       The Bronx
      </a>
      , north of Manhattan, despite the fact that the grid plan is not so regular there, and the last numbered street is 263rd Street.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308">
       <a href="#cite_note-308">
        [304]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Fifteen crosstown streets were designated as 100 feet (30 m) wide, including
      <a href="/wiki/34th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="34th Street (Manhattan)">
       34th
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/42nd_Street_(Manhattan)" title="42nd Street (Manhattan)">
       42nd
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/57th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="57th Street (Manhattan)">
       57th
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/125th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="125th Street (Manhattan)">
       125th
      </a>
      Streets,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309">
       <a href="#cite_note-309">
        [305]
       </a>
      </sup>
      which became some of the borough's most significant transportation and
      <a href="/wiki/Shopping" title="Shopping">
       shopping
      </a>
      venues.
      <a href="/wiki/Broadway_(Manhattan)" title="Broadway (Manhattan)">
       Broadway
      </a>
      is the most notable of many exceptions to the grid, starting at
      <a href="/wiki/Bowling_Green_(New_York_City)" title="Bowling Green (New York City)">
       Bowling Green
      </a>
      in Lower Manhattan and continuing north into the Bronx at Manhattan's northern tip. In much of Midtown Manhattan, Broadway runs at a diagonal to the grid, creating major named intersections at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Square_(New_York_City)" title="Union Square (New York City)">
       Union Square
      </a>
      (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Park_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Park Avenue (Manhattan)">
       Park Avenue South/Fourth Avenue
      </a>
      and 14th Street),
      <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square" title="Madison Square">
       Madison Square
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">
       Fifth Avenue
      </a>
      and 23rd Street),
      <a href="/wiki/Herald_Square" title="Herald Square">
       Herald Square
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Sixth Avenue (Manhattan)">
       Sixth Avenue
      </a>
      and 34th Street),
      <a href="/wiki/Times_Square" title="Times Square">
       Times Square
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Seventh Avenue (Manhattan)">
       Seventh Avenue
      </a>
      and 42nd Street), and
      <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Circle" title="Columbus Circle">
       Columbus Circle
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Avenue_(Manhattan)" title="Eighth Avenue (Manhattan)">
       Eighth Avenue
      </a>
      /
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Park_West" title="Central Park West">
       Central Park West
      </a>
      and 59th Street).
     </p>
     <p>
      "Crosstown traffic" refers primarily to vehicular traffic between
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan%27s_East_Side" title="Manhattan's East Side">
       Manhattan's East Side
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan%27s_West_Side" title="Manhattan's West Side">
       Manhattan's West Side
      </a>
      . The trip is notoriously frustrating for drivers because of heavy
      <a href="/wiki/Traffic_congestion" title="Traffic congestion">
       congestion
      </a>
      on narrow local streets laid out by the Commissioners' Plan of 1811, absence of express roads other than the
      <a href="/wiki/Trans-Manhattan_Expressway" title="Trans-Manhattan Expressway">
       Trans-Manhattan Expressway
      </a>
      at the far north end of Manhattan Island, and only 4 crosstown roads for travel through
      <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">
       Central Park
      </a>
      , which is between
      <a href="/wiki/59th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="59th Street (Manhattan)">
       59th Street
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/110th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="110th Street (Manhattan)">
       110th Street
      </a>
      . Proposals in the mid-1900s to build express roads through the city's densest neighborhoods, namely the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Manhattan_Expressway" title="Mid-Manhattan Expressway">
       Mid-Manhattan Expressway
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan_Expressway" title="Lower Manhattan Expressway">
       Lower Manhattan Expressway
      </a>
      , did not go forward. The congestion makes Manhattan's crosstown buses the perennial "winners" of the "
      <a href="/wiki/Straphangers_Campaign#Pokey_Awards" title="Straphangers Campaign">
       Pokey Awards
      </a>
      " for slowest service in New York City.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another consequence of the strict grid plan of most of Manhattan, and the grid's skew of approximately 28.9 degrees, is a phenomenon sometimes referred to as
      <a href="/wiki/Manhattanhenge" title="Manhattanhenge">
       Manhattanhenge
      </a>
      (by analogy with
      <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">
       Stonehenge
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manhattanhenge_310-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Manhattanhenge-310">
        [306]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On separate occasions in late May and early July, the sunset is aligned with the street grid lines, with the result that the sun is visible at or near the western horizon from street level.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manhattanhenge_310-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Manhattanhenge-310">
        [306]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311">
       <a href="#cite_note-311">
        [307]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A similar phenomenon occurs with the sunrise in January and December.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt_East_River_Drive" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt East River Drive">
       FDR Drive
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River_Drive" title="Harlem River Drive">
       Harlem River Drive
      </a>
      , both designed by controversial New York master planner
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Moses" title="Robert Moses">
       Robert Moses
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312">
       <a href="#cite_note-312">
        [308]
       </a>
      </sup>
      comprise a single, long
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limited-access_highway" title="Limited-access highway">
       limited-access
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parkway_(New_York)" title="Parkway (New York)">
       parkway
      </a>
      skirting the east side of Manhattan along the
      <a href="/wiki/East_River" title="East River">
       East River
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River" title="Harlem River">
       Harlem River
      </a>
      south of
      <a href="/wiki/Dyckman_Street" title="Dyckman Street">
       Dyckman Street
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_Hudson_Parkway" title="Henry Hudson Parkway">
       Henry Hudson Parkway
      </a>
      is the corresponding parkway on the West Side north of
      <a href="/wiki/57th_Street_(Manhattan)" title="57th Street (Manhattan)">
       57th Street
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="River_crossings">
       River crossings
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: River crossings">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hudson_River_(5422466822).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Hudson_River_%285422466822%29.jpg/250px-Hudson_River_%285422466822%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Hudson_River_%285422466822%29.jpg/375px-Hudson_River_%285422466822%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Hudson_River_%285422466822%29.jpg/500px-Hudson_River_%285422466822%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hudson_River_(5422466822).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        View Manhattan skylines, from
        <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">
         Hudson River
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Being primarily an island, Manhattan is linked to New York City's outer boroughs by numerous bridges, of various sizes. Manhattan has fixed
      <a href="/wiki/Highway" title="Highway">
       highway
      </a>
      connections with
      <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">
       New Jersey
      </a>
      to its west by way of the
      <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Bridge" title="George Washington Bridge">
       George Washington Bridge
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Holland_Tunnel" title="Holland Tunnel">
       Holland Tunnel
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Tunnel" title="Lincoln Tunnel">
       Lincoln Tunnel
      </a>
      , and to three of the four other New York City boroughs—
      <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">
       the Bronx
      </a>
      to the northeast, and
      <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">
       Brooklyn
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">
       Queens
      </a>
      (both on
      <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">
       Long Island
      </a>
      ) to the east and south. Its only direct connection with the fifth New York City borough,
      <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island" title="Staten Island">
       Staten Island
      </a>
      , is the
      <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island_Ferry" title="Staten Island Ferry">
       Staten Island Ferry
      </a>
      across
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_Harbor" title="New York Harbor">
       New York Harbor
      </a>
      , which is free of charge. The ferry terminal is located near
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battery_Park_(New_York)" title="Battery Park (New York)">
       Battery Park
      </a>
      at Manhattan's southern tip. It is also possible to travel on land to Staten Island by way of Brooklyn, via the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verrazano-Narrows_Bridge" title="Verrazano-Narrows Bridge">
       Verrazano-Narrows Bridge
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The George Washington Bridge, the world's busiest motor vehicle bridge,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-panynj.gov_313-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-panynj.gov-313">
        [309]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abcgwb_314-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-abcgwb-314">
        [310]
       </a>
      </sup>
      connects
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_Heights,_Manhattan" title="Washington Heights, Manhattan">
       Washington Heights
      </a>
      , in
      <a href="/wiki/Upper_Manhattan" title="Upper Manhattan">
       Upper Manhattan
      </a>
      , to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bergen_County" title="Bergen County">
       Bergen County
      </a>
      , in New Jersey. There are numerous bridges to the Bronx across the
      <a href="/wiki/Harlem_River" title="Harlem River">
       Harlem River
      </a>
      , and five of those (listed north to south)—the
      <a href="/wiki/Triborough_Bridge" title="Triborough Bridge">
       Triborough
      </a>
      (known officially as the Robert F. Kennedy Bridge),
      <a href="/wiki/Queensboro_Bridge" title="Queensboro Bridge">
       Ed Koch Queensboro
      </a>
      (also known as the 59th Street Bridge),
      <a href="/wiki/Williamsburg_Bridge" title="Williamsburg Bridge">
       Williamsburg
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Bridge" title="Manhattan Bridge">
       Manhattan
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Bridge" title="Brooklyn Bridge">
       Brooklyn Bridges
      </a>
      —connect Manhattan to Long Island.
     </p>
     <p>
      Several tunnels also link Manhattan Island to New York City's outer boroughs and New Jersey. The
      <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Tunnel" title="Lincoln Tunnel">
       Lincoln Tunnel
      </a>
      , which carries 120,000 vehicles a day under the Hudson River between New Jersey and
      <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">
       Midtown Manhattan
      </a>
      , is the busiest vehicular tunnel in the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315">
       <a href="#cite_note-315">
        [311]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The tunnel was built instead of a bridge to allow unfettered passage of large passenger and
      <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">
       cargo ships
      </a>
      that sail through New York Harbor and up the Hudson River to Manhattan's piers. The
      <a href="/wiki/Holland_Tunnel" title="Holland Tunnel">
       Holland Tunnel
      </a>
      , connecting Lower Manhattan to
      <a href="/wiki/Jersey_City,_New_Jersey" title="Jersey City, New Jersey">
       Jersey City, New Jersey
      </a>
      , was the world's first mechanically ventilated vehicular tunnel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316">
       <a href="#cite_note-316">
        [312]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queens-Midtown_Tunnel" title="Queens-Midtown Tunnel">
       Queens-Midtown Tunnel
      </a>
      , built to relieve congestion on the bridges connecting Manhattan with Queens and Brooklyn, was the largest non-federal project in its time when it was completed in 1940;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317">
       <a href="#cite_note-317">
        [313]
       </a>
      </sup>
      President
      <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">
       Franklin D. Roosevelt
      </a>
      was the first person to drive through it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318">
       <a href="#cite_note-318">
        [314]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn%E2%80%93Battery_Tunnel" title="Brooklyn–Battery Tunnel">
       Brooklyn–Battery Tunnel
      </a>
      runs underneath
      <a href="/wiki/Battery_Park" title="Battery Park">
       Battery Park
      </a>
      and connects the
      <a href="/wiki/Financial_District,_Manhattan" title="Financial District, Manhattan">
       Financial District
      </a>
      at the southern tip of Manhattan to
      <a href="/wiki/Red_Hook,_Brooklyn" title="Red Hook, Brooklyn">
       Red Hook
      </a>
      in Brooklyn.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Heliports">
       Heliports
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Heliports">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Manhattan has three public heliports: the
      <a href="/wiki/East_34th_Street_Heliport" title="East 34th Street Heliport">
       East 34th Street Heliport
      </a>
      (also known as the Atlantic Metroport) at East 34th Street, owned by New York City and run by the
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Economic_Development_Corporation" title="New York City Economic Development Corporation">
       New York City Economic Development Corporation
      </a>
      ("NYCEDC")); the
      <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Manhattan_Heliport" title="Downtown Manhattan Heliport">
       Port Authority Downtown Manhattan/Wall Street Heliport
      </a>
      , owned by the
      <a href="/wiki/Port_Authority_of_New_York_and_New_Jersey" title="Port Authority of New York and New Jersey">
       Port Authority of New York and New Jersey
      </a>
      and run by the NYCEDC; and the
      <a href="/wiki/West_30th_Street_Heliport" title="West 30th Street Heliport">
       West 30th Street Heliport
      </a>
      , a privately owned heliport that is owned by the Hudson River Park Trust.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319">
       <a href="#cite_note-319">
        [315]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/US_Helicopter" title="US Helicopter">
       US Helicopter
      </a>
      offered regularly scheduled helicopter service connecting the
      <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Manhattan_Heliport" title="Downtown Manhattan Heliport">
       Downtown Manhattan Heliport
      </a>
      with
      <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_International_Airport" title="John F. Kennedy International Airport">
       John F. Kennedy International Airport
      </a>
      in Queens and
      <a href="/wiki/Newark_Liberty_International_Airport" title="Newark Liberty International Airport">
       Newark Liberty International Airport
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">
       New Jersey
      </a>
      , before going out of business in 2009.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320">
       <a href="#cite_note-320">
        [316]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Utilities">
       Utilities
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Utilities">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Gas and electric service is provided by
      <a href="/wiki/Consolidated_Edison" title="Consolidated Edison">
       Consolidated Edison
      </a>
      to all of Manhattan. Con Edison's electric business traces its roots back to
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edison" title="Thomas Edison">
       Thomas Edison
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Edison_Illuminating_Company" title="Edison Illuminating Company">
       Edison Electric Illuminating Company
      </a>
      , the first investor-owned electric utility. The company started service on September 4, 1882, using one generator to provide 110
      <a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">
       volts
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">
       direct current
      </a>
      (DC) to 59 customers with 800 light bulbs, in a one-square-mile area of Lower Manhattan from his
      <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Street_Station" title="Pearl Street Station">
       Pearl Street Station
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321">
       <a href="#cite_note-321">
        [317]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Con_Edison_steam_operations" title="Con Edison steam operations">
       Con Edison operates
      </a>
      the world's largest
      <a href="/wiki/District_heating" title="District heating">
       district steam
      </a>
      system, which consists of 105 miles (169 km) of steam pipes, providing steam for heating, hot water, and air conditioning
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322">
       <a href="#cite_note-322">
        [318]
       </a>
      </sup>
      by some 1,800 Manhattan customers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323">
       <a href="#cite_note-323">
        [319]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cable service is provided by
      <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner_Cable" title="Time Warner Cable">
       Time Warner Cable
      </a>
      and telephone service is provided by
      <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Communications" title="Verizon Communications">
       Verizon Communications
      </a>
      , although
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AT%26T_Inc." title="AT&amp;T Inc.">
       AT&amp;T
      </a>
      is available as well.
     </p>
     <p>
      Manhattan, surrounded by two
      <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">
       brackish
      </a>
      rivers, had a limited supply of
      <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">
       fresh water
      </a>
      . To satisfy the growing population, the city of New York acquired land in
      <a href="/wiki/Westchester_County,_New_York" title="Westchester County, New York">
       Westchester County
      </a>
      and constructed the old
      <a href="/wiki/Croton_Aqueduct" title="Croton Aqueduct">
       Croton Aqueduct
      </a>
      system, which went into service in 1842 and was superseded by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Croton_Aqueduct" title="New Croton Aqueduct">
       new Croton Aqueduct
      </a>
      , which opened in 1890, but this was interrupted in 2008 for the construction since underway of a US$3.2 billion water purification plant that can supply an estimated 290 million gallons daily when completed, representing almost a 20% addition to the city's availability of water, with this addition going to Manhattan and the Bronx.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324">
       <a href="#cite_note-324">
        [320]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Water comes to Manhattan through
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_City_Water_Tunnel_No._1" title="New York City Water Tunnel No. 1">
       New York City Water Tunnel No. 1
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_City_Water_Tunnel_No._2" title="New York City Water Tunnel No. 2">
       Tunnel No. 2
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Water_Tunnel_No._3" title="New York City Water Tunnel No. 3">
       Tunnel No. 3
      </a>
      , completed in 1917, 1936, and (Manhattan's supply) 2013,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325">
       <a href="#cite_note-325">
        [321]
       </a>
      </sup>
      respectively.
     </p>
     <p>
      Manhattan witnessed the doubling of the
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">
       natural gas
      </a>
      supply delivered to the borough when a new
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_pipeline" title="Gas pipeline">
       gas pipeline
      </a>
      opened on November 1, 2013.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326">
       <a href="#cite_note-326">
        [322]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Department_of_Sanitation" title="New York City Department of Sanitation">
       New York City Department of Sanitation
      </a>
      is responsible for garbage removal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327">
       <a href="#cite_note-327">
        [323]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The bulk of the city's trash ultimately is disposed at mega-dumps in Pennsylvania, Virginia, South Carolina and Ohio (via transfer stations in New Jersey, Brooklyn and Queens) since the 2001 closure of the
      <a href="/wiki/Fresh_Kills_Landfill" title="Fresh Kills Landfill">
       Fresh Kills Landfill
      </a>
      on
      <a href="/wiki/Staten_Island" title="Staten Island">
       Staten Island
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328">
       <a href="#cite_note-328">
        [324]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A small amount of trash processed at transfer sites in New Jersey is sometimes incinerated at
      <a href="/wiki/Waste-to-energy" title="Waste-to-energy">
       waste-to-energy
      </a>
      facilities. Like New York City, New Jersey and much of Greater New York relies on exporting its trash to far-flung areas.
     </p>
     <p>
      New York City has the largest clean-air diesel-
      <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">
       hybrid
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">
       compressed natural gas
      </a>
      bus fleet, which also operates in Manhattan, in the country. It also has some of the first hybrid taxis, most of which operate in Manhattan.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329">
       <a href="#cite_note-329">
        [325]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Address_algorithm">
       Address algorithm
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Manhattan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Address algorithm">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_address_algorithm" title="Manhattan address algorithm">
       Manhattan address algorithm
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_address_algorithm" title="Manhattan address algorithm">
       address algorithm of Manhattan
      </a>
      is commonly used to find an address on a major street in Manhattan. It is commonly found in telephone directories, New York City guide books, and
      <a href="/wiki/MTA_Regional_Bus_Operations" title="MTA Regional Bus Operations">
       MTA Manhattan bus
      </a>
      maps.
     </p>

       <li id="cite_note-SciAm-40">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-SciAm_40-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>

        <span class="reference-text">

         </a>

         , September 25, 1909, accessed May 1, 2007. "This was a vain hope however, and the conviction must finally have come to the heart of the intrepid adventurer that once again he was foiled in his repeated quest for the northwest passage ... On the following day the "Half Moon" let go her anchor inside of Sandy Hook. The week was spent in exploring the bay with a shallop, or small boat, and "they found a good entrance between two headlands" (the Narrows) "and thus entered on the September 11 into as fine a river as can be found.""
        </span>
       </li>

         Which existed for many reasons, including the industrial power of the Hudson River harbor, which allowed trade with stops such as the West Point Foundry one of the great manufacturing hubs of the early US, and the NYC Atlantic Ocean ports, New York City was the American industrial powerhouse in terms of industrial trade between north and south.

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AManhattan&amp;rft.btitle=Manhattan+Schist+in+New+York+City+Parks+%93+J.+Hood+Wright+Park&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.nycgovparks.org%2Fparks%2Fjhoodwrightpark%2Fhighlights%2F12369&amp;rft.pub=New+York+City+Department+of+Parks+%26+Recreation&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-112">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-112">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation web">
          Quinn, Helen (June 6, 2013).
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/science-environment-22798563" rel="nofollow">
           "How ancient collision shaped New York skyline"
          </a>
          .
          <i>
           BBC Science
          </i>
          . BBC.co.uk
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            June 13,
           </span>
           2013
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           These rocks are Manhattan schist, part of that ancient supercontinent, fragments of Pangaea left behind when the continent split. They are just glimpses of what is below the surface in abundance in Downtown and Midtown. And it is these fragments of very hard rock that provide the perfect foundations for New York's highest buildings. Where Manhattan schist can be found very close to the surface you can build high, and so Downtown and Midtown have become home to Manhattan's tallest buildings.
          </q>
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AManhattan&amp;rft.atitle=How+ancient+collision+shaped+New+York+skyline&amp;rft.aufirst=Helen&amp;rft.aulast=Quinn&amp;rft.date=2013-06-06&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.bbc.co.uk%2Fnews%2Fscience-environment-22798563&amp;rft.jtitle=BBC+Science&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>

       <li id="cite_note-165">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-165">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Gray, Christopher.
         <a class="external text" href="http://query.nytimes.com/gst/fullpage.html?res=9F0CE4DA1739F931A35752C0A9639C8B63&amp;sec=&amp;spon=&amp;pagewanted=print" rel="nofollow">
          " Streetscapes/Singer Building; Once the Tallest Building, But Since 1967 a Ghost"
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          The New York Times
         </i>
         , January 2, 2005. Accessed May 15, 2007. "The 41-story Singer Building, the tallest in the world in 1908 when it was completed at Broadway and Liberty Street, was, until September 11, 2001, the tallest structure ever to be demolished. The building, an elegant Beaux-Arts tower, was one of the most painful losses of the early preservation movement when it was razed in 1967.... Begun in 1906, the Singer Building incorporated Flagg's model for
         <i>
          a city of towers
         </i>
         , with the 1896 structure reconstructed as the base, and a 65-foot-square shaft rising 612 feet (187 m) high, culminating in a bulbous mansard and giant lantern at the peak."
        </span>
       </li>

       <li id="cite_note-170">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-170">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Gray, Christopher.
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.nytimes.com/1995/12/17/realestate/streetscapes-the-chrysler-building-skyscraper-s-place-in-the-sun.html" rel="nofollow">
          "Streetscapes: The Chrysler Building; Skyscraper's Place in the Sun"
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          The New York Times
         </i>
         , December 17, 1995. Accessed June 30, 2009. "Then Chrysler and Van Alen again revised the design, this time in order to win a height competition with the 921-foot (281 m) tower then rising at 40 Wall Street. This was done in secret, using as a staging area the huge square fire-tower shaft, intended to vent smoke from the stairways. Inside the shaft, Van Alen had teams of workers assemble the framework for a 185-foot-high spire that, when lifted into place in the fall of 1929, made the Chrysler building, at 1046 feet, 4.75 inches high, the tallest in the world."
        </span>

        <span class="reference-text">

         <a class="external text" href="http://www.nytimes.com/1992/06/14/realestate/streetscapes-the-empire-state-building-a-red-reprise-for-a-31-wonder.html?n=Top%2FReference%2FTimes%20Topics%2FSubjects%2FE%2FEmpire%20State%20Building" rel="nofollow">
          "Streetscapes: The Empire State Building; A Red Reprise for a '31 Wonder"
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          The New York Times
         </i>
         , June 14, 1992. Accessed June 30, 2009.

       <li id="cite_note-173">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-173">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Barss, Karen.
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.infoplease.com/spot/skyscraperhistory.html" rel="nofollow">
          "The History of Skyscrapers: A race to the top"
         </a>
         , Information Please. Accessed May 17, 2007. "The Empire State Building would reign supreme among skyscrapers for 41 years until 1972, when it was surpassed by the World Trade Center (1,368 feet, 110 stories). Two years later, New York City lost the distinction of housing the tallest building when the Willis Tower was constructed in Chicago (1450 feet, 110 stories)."

        </span>

          "The Melting Pot"
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          The First Measured Century
         </i>
         ,

       <li id="cite_note-243">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-243">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Dolkart, Andrew S.
         <a class="external text" href="http://ci.columbia.edu/0240s/0242_2/0242_2_s5_text.html" rel="nofollow">
          "The Architecture and Development of New York City: The Birth of the Skyscraper – Romantic Symbols"
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">
          Columbia University
         </a>
         , accessed May 15, 2007. "It is at a triangular site where Broadway and Fifth Avenue—the two most important streets of New York—meet at Madison Square, and because of the juxtaposition of the streets and the park across the street, there was a wind-tunnel effect here. In the early twentieth century, men would hang out on the corner here on Twenty-third Street and watch the wind blowing women's dresses up so that they could catch a little bit of ankle. This entered into popular culture and there are hundreds of postcards and illustrations of women with their dresses blowing up in front of the Flatiron Building. And it supposedly is where the slang expression "23 skidoo" comes from because the police would come and give the voyeurs the 23 skidoo to tell them to get out of the area."
        </span>
       </li>

       <li id="cite_note-282">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-282">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.virtualny.cuny.edu/FIRE/greatfire1776.html" rel="nofollow">
          Great Fire of 1776
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/City_University_of_New_York" title="City University of New York">
          City University of New York
         </a>
         . Accessed April 30, 2007. "Some of Washington's advisors suggested burning New York City so that the British would gain little from its capture. This idea was abandoned and Washington withdrew his forces from the city on September 12, 1776. Three days later the British occupied the city and on September 21, a fire broke out in the Fighting Cocks Tavern. Without the city's firemen present and on duty, the fire quickly spread. A third of the city burnt and 493 houses destroyed."

           ^

          "New York City Pedestrian Level of Service Study – Phase I, 2006"

"In the year 2000, 88% of workers over 16 years old in the U.S. used a car, truck or van to commute to work, while approximately 5% used public transportation and 3% walked to work.... In Manhattan, the borough with the highest population density (66,940 people/sq mi. in year 2000; 1,564,798 inhabitants) and concentration of business and tourist destinations, only 18% of the working population drove to work in 2000, while 72% used public transportation and 8% walked."

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AManhattan&amp;rft.btitle=New+York+City+Map&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fgis.nyc.gov%2Fdoitt%2Fnycitymap%2F&amp;rft.pub=NYC.gov&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-309">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-309">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.library.cornell.edu/Reps/DOCS/nyc1811.htm" rel="nofollow">
          Remarks of the Commissioners for laying out streets and roads in the City of New York, under the Act of April 3, 1807
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">
          Cornell University
         </a>
         . Accessed May 2, 2007. "These streets are all sixty feet wide except fifteen, which are one hundred feet wide, viz.: Numbers fourteen, twenty-three, thirty-four, forty-two, fifty-seven, seventy-two, seventy-nine, eighty-six, ninety-six, one hundred and six, one hundred and sixteen, one hundred and twenty-five, one hundred and thirty-five, one hundred and forty-five, and one hundred and fifty-five—the block or space between them being in general about two hundred feet."

       <li id="cite_note-Manhattanhenge-310">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Manhattanhenge_310-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Manhattanhenge_310-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <cite class="citation news">
          Silverman, Justin Rocket (May 27, 2006).
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.newsday.com/news/sunny-delight-in-city-sight-1.502140" rel="nofollow">
           "Sunny delight in city sight"
          </a>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/Newsday" title="Newsday">
           Newsday
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           'Manhattanhenge' occurs Sunday, a day when a happy coincidence of urban planning and astrophysics results in the setting sun lining up exactly with every east-west street in the borough north of 14th Street. Similar to Stonehenge, which is directly aligned with the summer-solstice sun, "Manhattanhenge" catches the sun descending in perfect alignment between buildings. The local phenomenon occurs twice a year, on May 28 and July 12...
          </q>
         </cite>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AManhattan&amp;rft.atitle=Sunny+delight+in+city+sight&amp;rft.aufirst=Justin+Rocket&amp;rft.aulast=Silverman&amp;rft.date=2006-05-27&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.newsday.com%2Fnews%2Fsunny-delight-in-city-sight-1.502140&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>

        </i>

          Towers of Secrecy
         </a>
         .
        </i>
        A series of 6 articles "examining people behind shell companies buying high-end real estate" in midtown Manhattan. Part 1: Time Warner Center: Symbol of the Boom,   Part 2: The Mysterious Malaysian Financier,   Part 3: The Besieged Indian Builder,   Part 4: The Mexican Power Brokers,   Part 5: The Russian Minister and Friends,   Summary: The Hidden Money Buying Up New York Real Estate.
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">
          The New York Times
         </a>
        </i>
